<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A footprint of desiccation tolerance in the genome of Xerophyta viscosa"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2017 3:4"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-03-27"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 Macmillan Publishers Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Desiccation tolerance is common in seeds and various other organisms, but only a few angiosperm species possess vegetative desiccation tolerance. These &#8216;resurrection species&#8217; may serve as ideal models for the ultimate design of crops with enhanced drought tolerance. To understand the molecular and genetic mechanisms enabling vegetative desiccation tolerance, we produced a high-quality whole-genome sequence for the resurrection plant Xerophyta viscosa and assessed transcriptome changes during its dehydration. Data revealed induction of transcripts typically associated with desiccation tolerance in seeds and involvement of orthologues of ABI3 and ABI5, both key regulators of seed maturation. Dehydration resulted in both increased, but predominantly reduced, transcript abundance of genomic &#8216;clusters of desiccation-associated genes&#8217; (CoDAGs), reflecting the cessation of growth that allows for the expression of desiccation tolerance. Vegetative desiccation tolerance in X. viscosa was found to be uncoupled from drought-induced senescence. We provide strong support for the hypothesis that vegetative desiccation tolerance arose by redirection of genetic information from desiccation-tolerant seeds. The mechanism underlying vegetative desiccation tolerance (DT) of plants remains elusive. A study now sequences the genome and transcriptome for the resurrection plant, Xerophyta viscosa, and supports that vegetative DT arose by redirection of the seed DT pathway."/>

Jiménez-Gómez</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Max Planck Institute for Plant Breeding Research, Carl-von-Linné-Weg 10, 50829 Cologne" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.419498.9, 0000 0001 0660 6765, Department of Plant Breeding and Genetics, Max Planck Institute for Plant Breeding Research, Carl-von-Linné-Weg 10, 50829 Cologne, Germany" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Thamara-Hesselink" data-author-popup="auth-Thamara-Hesselink">Thamara Hesselink</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Bioscience, Wageningen Plant Research International, Droevendaalsesteeg 1, 6708PB Wageningen" /><meta itemprop="address" content="Bioscience, Wageningen Plant Research International, Droevendaalsesteeg 1, 6708PB Wageningen, The Netherlands" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Elio_G__W__M_-Schijlen" data-author-popup="auth-Elio_G__W__M_-Schijlen">Elio G.             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-0228-169X"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-0228-169X</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Laboratory of Plant Physiology, Wageningen University, Droevendaalsesteeg 1, 6708PB Wageningen" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.4818.5, 0000 0001 0791 5666, Laboratory of Plant Physiology, Wageningen University, Droevendaalsesteeg 1, 6708PB Wageningen, The Netherlands" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Melvin_J_-Oliver" data-author-popup="auth-Melvin_J_-Oliver">Melvin J. Oliver</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff7">7</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="USDA-ARS-MWA-PGRU, 205 Curtis Hall, University of Missouri" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.134936.a, 0000 0001 2162 3504, USDA-ARS-MWA-PGRU, 205 Curtis Hall, University of Missouri, Columbia, 65211, Missouri, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jill_M_-Farrant" data-author-popup="auth-Jill_M_-Farrant">Jill M. Farrant</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Cape Town, Private Bag, 7701 Cape Town" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.7836.a, 0000 0004 1937 1151, Department of Molecular and Cell Biology, University of Cape Town, Private Bag, 7701 Cape Town, South Africa" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Henk_W__M_-Hilhorst" data-author-popup="auth-Henk_W__M_-Hilhorst" data-corresp-id="c1">Henk W.             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Desiccation tolerance is common in seeds and various other organisms, but only a few angiosperm species possess vegetative desiccation tolerance. These ‘resurrection species’ may serve as ideal models for the ultimate design of crops with enhanced drought tolerance. To understand the molecular and genetic mechanisms enabling vegetative desiccation tolerance, we produced a high-quality whole-genome sequence for the resurrection plant <i>Xerophyta viscos</i>a and assessed transcriptome changes during its dehydration. Data revealed induction of transcripts typically associated with desiccation tolerance in seeds and involvement of orthologues of ABI3 and ABI5, both key regulators of seed maturation. Dehydration resulted in both increased, but predominantly reduced, transcript abundance of genomic ‘clusters of desiccation-associated genes’ (CoDAGs), reflecting the cessation of growth that allows for the expression of desiccation tolerance. Vegetative desiccation tolerance in <i>X. viscosa</i> was found to be uncoupled from drought-induced senescence. We provide strong support for the hypothesis that vegetative desiccation tolerance arose by redirection of genetic information from desiccation-tolerant seeds.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>

                    <div class="c-nature-box c-nature-box--side u-display-none" aria-hidden="true" data-component="entitlement-box" id=entitlement-box-mobile>
    
        <p class="c-nature-box__text js-text u-display-none" aria-hidden="true"></p>
        
    
    <div class="c-pdf-download u-clear-both">
        <a href="/articles/nplants201738.pdf" class="c-pdf-download__link" data-article-pdf="true" data-readcube-pdf-url="true" data-test="download-pdf" data-draft-ignore="true" data-track="click" data-track-action="download pdf" data-track-label="link" data-track-external download>
            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

    
</div>

                
                
                    <div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The grass family (Poaceae) is arguably the most important contributor to global food security. However, poaceous staple crops, such as maize, corn, rice and wheat, do not survive the extreme water loss that is inevitably brought about by extended periods of drought. In contrast, their seeds are desiccation tolerant, and they can be dried to water contents as low as 1–5% on a fresh weight basis without losing viability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kermode, A. R. Approaches to elucidate the basis of desiccation-tolerance in seeds. Seed Sci. Res. 7, 75–95 (1997)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41924e644">1</a></sup>. There are some 135 angiosperm species, termed ‘resurrection plants’, which produce desiccation-tolerant seeds and possess desiccation-tolerant vegetative tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Black, M. &amp; Pritchard, H. W. (eds) in Desiccation and Survival in Plants 207–237 (CABI, 2002); 
                    http://www.cabi.org/cabebooks/ebook/20023069464
                    
                  " href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d41924e648">2</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Porembski, S. in Plant Desiccation Tolerance Vol. 215 (eds Lüttge, U., Beck, E. &amp; Bartels, D. ) 139–156 (Springer, 2011)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d41924e651">4</a></sup>. Vegetative desiccation tolerance (DT) first arose with the transition from aquatic to terrestrial life forms, when both the probability of experiencing adverse conditions and the survival cost of such conditions were high<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jönsson, K. I. &amp; Järemo, J. A model on the evolution of cryptobiosis. Ann. Zool. Fennici. 40, 331–34040 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d41924e655">5</a></sup>. As plants expanded into terrestrial habitats and developed tracheids to move water from the substrate to their aerial parts and more complex ecosystems were established, the slow growth characteristic of desiccation-tolerant plants was limiting their competitive ability. This favoured the loss of DT in vegetative tissues, the development of mechanisms to prevent water loss (physiological and morphological) and the confinement of DT to seeds, spores and pollen grains where it was required for dispersal and preservation of genetic resources<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Alpert, P. Constraints of tolerance: why are desiccation-tolerant organisms so small or rare? J. Exp. Biol. 209, 1575–1584 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d41924e659">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Oliver, M. J., Tuba, Z. &amp; Mishler, B. D. The evolution of vegetative desiccation tolerance in land plants. Plant Ecol. 151, 85–100 (2000)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d41924e662">7</a></sup>. It has been proposed that vegetative DT reappeared in the angiosperms, presumably in response to colonization of environmentally demanding habitats, in at least 13 separate lineages to evolve the present day resurrection plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Porembski, S. in Plant Desiccation Tolerance Vol. 215 (eds Lüttge, U., Beck, E. &amp; Bartels, D. ) 139–156 (Springer, 2011)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d41924e666">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Oliver, M. J., Tuba, Z. &amp; Mishler, B. D. The evolution of vegetative desiccation tolerance in land plants. Plant Ecol. 151, 85–100 (2000)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d41924e669">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Oliver, M. J., Velten, J. &amp; Mishler, B. D. Desiccation tolerance in bryophytes: a reflection of the primitive strategy for plant survival in dehydrating habitats? Integr. Comp. Biol. 45, 788–799 (2005)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d41924e672">8</a></sup>.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The myriad genetic changes experienced in the evolution of resurrection plants that enabled vegetative DT are not completely understood. An improved understanding of these changes will aid the development of crop improvement strategies for tolerance of water loss and survival of extreme drought conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Farrant, J. M. et al. A molecular physiological review of vegetative desiccation tolerance in the resurrection plant Xerophyta viscosa (Baker). Planta 242, 407–426 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d41924e679">9</a></sup>. The pressure to develop such crop varieties has been intensified by predictions of a near future with increased drought and declining water resources in the world's main agricultural areas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Dai, A. Increasing drought under global warming in observations and models. Nat. Clim. Change 3, 52–58 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d41924e683">10</a></sup>. This pressure is strongest in developing countries, which struggle to maintain robust breeding capabilities and need support to develop stress-tolerant crops critical for food security<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Farrant, J. M. et al. A molecular physiological review of vegetative desiccation tolerance in the resurrection plant Xerophyta viscosa (Baker). Planta 242, 407–426 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d41924e687">9</a></sup>. In this context, the study of resurrection plants, such as <i>Xerophyta viscosa</i> (Velloziaceae), will bring valuable information to bear on improving stress tolerance in crops. <i>X. viscosa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>) is a monocotyledonous plant species and, thus, phylogenetically closely related to staple cereal crops. It is thus an ideal model for understanding plant requisites to tolerate extreme dehydration.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="X. viscosa phenotypes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: <i>X. viscosa</i> phenotypes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201738/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2017.38/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants201738_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2017.38/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants201738_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="625"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The chasmophytic nature of growth in the natural environment. <b>b</b>, A typical fully dehydrated plant (withholding water from the whole plant over a period of 25 days until &lt;5% RWC). <b>c</b>, A rehydrated plant (at full turgor after 5 days of watering). Scale bars, 10 cm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201738/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>We have produced a high-quality whole-genome sequence and assembly for <i>X. viscosa</i> from a mixed dataset of Illumina and Pac-Bio reads along with a full assessment of the transcriptomal changes that occur in young seedlings in response to exogenous abscisic acid (ABA) and in adult plants during desiccation and rehydration.</p></div><section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="A high-quality octoploid genome assembly"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">A high-quality octoploid genome assembly</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The source of genomic DNA consisted of individuals (five for Illumina and one for PacBio sequencing) grown from seeds harvested from a population of <i>X. viscosa</i> collected from the Buffelskloof Nature Reserve (Mpumalanga Province, South Africa). <i>X. viscosa</i> has a high level of heterozygosity due to self-incompatibility. High levels of heterozygosity could cause several haplotypes to be assembled in different contigs causing duplicates in the final haploid assembly. However, we used Falcon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d41924e751">11</a></sup> which is a tool designed to deal with high levels of heterozygosity and, thus, no measures were taken to reduce genome complexity. The genome was sequenced and assembled<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Song, L., Florea, L. &amp; Langmead, B. Lighter: fast and memory-efficient sequencing error correction without counting. Genome Biol. 15, 509 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d41924e755">12</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Boetzer, M. et al. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 211 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d41924e758">15</a></sup> using a whole-genome shotgun approach which combined 23 Gb of raw paired-end Illumina reads (<span class="stix">∼</span>77× coverage) and 17.5 Gb of PacBio long reads (<span class="stix">∼</span>58× coverage). This high-quality assembly covers 99.8% of the genome and consists of 1,811 contigs and 896 scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Tab1">Table 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Properties of the <i>X. viscosa</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nplants201738/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A k-mer analysis of PacBio data indicated a (haploid) genome size of 295.5 Mb. Based on allele frequencies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Yoshida, K. et al. The rise and fall of the Phytophthora infestans lineage that triggered the Irish potato famine. eLife 2, e00731 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1859">16</a></sup>, we determined the <i>X. viscosa</i> genome to be an octoploid (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Polyploidy has been associated with vigour and size in crops, has been proposed to facilitate better environmental adaptability and is regarded as one of the major drivers of speciation, and thus evolution. It follows that polyploid species may be more competitive and invasive in new environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="de Melo, N. F. et al. Cytogenetics and cytotaxonomy of velloziaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 204, 257–273 (1997)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1869">17</a></sup>. The African Velloziaceae species are all polyploid and assumed to have originated on the South-American continent, where family members are largely diploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="de Melo, N. F. et al. Cytogenetics and cytotaxonomy of velloziaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 204, 257–273 (1997)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1873">17</a></sup>. Thus, polyploidization may have contributed to successful invasion of the Velloziaceae in southern Africa. Our data indicates that members of the Velloziaceae may be octoploids and not hexaploids, which is the current consensus of opinion.</p><p><i>X. viscosa</i> has 48 small chromosomes (estimated length of 1.5–2.0 microns, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). We assembled a 295.5 Mb haploid genome and 90% of the assembly is contained in 238 scaffolds. The scaffold N50 (the scaffold size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly can be found) is 1.67 Mb, which is very high for an octoploid genome. It approaches the N50 of the diploid resurrection species <i>Oropetium thomaeum</i> (N50 2.4 Mb, genome size 245 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508–511 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1888">18</a></sup> and is much greater than the N50 of the diploid resurrection species <i>Boea hygrometrica</i> (N50 110 kb, <span class="stix">∼</span>1,691-Mb sequenced genome)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xiao, L. et al. The resurrection genome of Boea hygrometrica: a blueprint for survival of dehydration. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 5833–5837 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1895">19</a></sup> or the tetraploid <i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> (N50 87 kb, genome size 1,448 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Yasui, Y. et al. Draft genome sequence of an inbred line of Chenopodium quinoa, an allotetraploid crop with great environmental adaptability and outstanding nutritional properties. DNA Res. 23, 535–546 (2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1903">20</a></sup>. In addition to the 295 Mb final assembly, we assembled 125 Mb of additional haplotypes in 6,103 contigs.</p><p>The GC content is 36.5% across the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Greater GC contents have been associated with species that grow in seasonally cold and/or dry climates, perhaps suggesting an advantage of GC-rich DNA during cell freezing and desiccation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Šmarda, P. et al. Ecological and evolutionary significance of genomic GC content diversity in monocots. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4096–E4102 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1913">21</a></sup>. Whereas <i>B. hygrometrica</i> has a relatively high GC content of 42.30%, it is only 34.86% in <i>O. thomaeum</i>, thus not supporting a general positive correlation between DT and GC content.</p><p>A total of 25,425 protein-coding genes were annotated; 97% of the encoded proteins exhibit high sequence similarity to proteins in the TrEMBL database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1927">22</a></sup>, 85% in Swiss-Prot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1931">22</a></sup> and 84% in InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Mitchell, A. et al. The InterPro protein families database: the classification resource after 15 years. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D213–D221 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1935">23</a></sup>. The percentage of orphan genes (5.4%, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Tab1">Table 1</a>), or genes that do not share any similarity with genes in other species, is considerably lower than expected for eukaryotic genomes (10–20%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wilson, G. A. et al. Orphans as taxonomically restricted and ecologically important genes. Microbiology 151, 2499–2501 (2005)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1942">24</a></sup>. This low percentage may suggest that the acquisition of vegetative DT by <i>X. viscosa</i> relied more on the redirection of genetic information than on the genesis of novel genes.</p><p>Transposable elements (TEs) account for 18% of the genome, which is a surprisingly low number (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Tab1">Table 1</a>) considering that TEs in the genomes of <i>O. thomaeum</i> and <i>B. hygrometrica</i> account for 75% and 43%, respectively. Our high-quality data rules out collapsed or incomplete sequence assemblies. Plant TEs have been proposed to regulate DT in the dicot resurrection species <i>Craterostigma plantagineum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Hilbricht, T. et al. Retrotransposons and siRNA have a role in the evolution of desiccation tolerance leading to resurrection of the plant Craterostigma plantagineum. New Phytol. 179, 877–887 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1964">25</a></sup>. Taken together, the proportion of TEs in a plant genome does not appear to be related to DT in general.</p><p>Orthologous clustering of the <i>X. viscosa</i> proteome with 15 plant genomes identified 15,450 orthologous groups (OGs) in common, with 20 OGs present specifically in <i>X. viscosa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The OGs were used to map signatures of expansions and contractions of gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Overall, <i>X. viscosa</i> did not undergo extensive expansions or contractions of gene families. Although many gene families were shared between <i>X. viscosa</i> and other monocots, such as the desiccation-sensitive but relatively drought-tolerant <i>Eragrostis tef</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Cannarozzi, G. et al. Genome and transcriptome sequencing identifies breeding targets in the orphan crop tef (Eragrostis tef). BMC Genomics 15, 581 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d41924e1992">26</a></sup> (<i>X. viscosa</i> and <i>E. tef</i> share 70% of OGs) and the resurrection plant <i>O. thomaeum</i> (<i>X. viscosa</i> and <i>O. thomaeum</i> share 72% of OGs), clearly there are several expansions and contractions unique to <i>X. viscosa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). The <i>X. viscosa</i> exclusive expansions include several gene families associated with cellular stress and metabolic regulation, such as putative late embryogenesis abundant (LEA) proteins, heat shock domain-containing proteins, putative WRKY transcription-factors and MADS-box domain-containing proteins. The contractions unique to <i>X. viscosa</i> include gene families related to control of gene expression, such as plastid transcriptionally active chromosome proteins and DNA helicases. The low overlap in expanded or contracted gene families between <i>X. viscosa</i> and <i>O. thomaeum</i> may indicate different genetic architectures underlying the resurrection phenotype observed in both species and hint at independent evolution to reacquire vegetative DT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gaff, D. F. &amp; Oliver, M. J. The evolution of desiccation tolerance in angiosperm plants: a rare yet common phenomenon. Funct. Plant Biol. 40, 315–328 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2031">27</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Resurrection physiology"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Resurrection physiology</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p><i>X. viscosa</i> seeds and adult plants are desiccation tolerant. However, individuals are not continuously desiccation tolerant from seed to adult plant. They lose DT briefly upon germination, which recovers gradually during seedling development, first in shoots and later in roots. This recovery of DT can be induced earlier in shoots, but not in roots, by application of ABA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). In shoots, exogenous ABA induced the accumulation of transcripts of genes involved in chlorophyll degradation, translational control of gene expression and transport, whereas it decreased transcript abundance of genes related to photosynthesis, energy metabolism and ABA biosynthesis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 2</a>). In roots, transcript abundance of genes involved in chlorophyll degradation and responsiveness to brassinosteroids increased, whereas transcripts of genes related to energy metabolism and plant responses to external signals (mainly by modifying cell walls) were depleted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><p>In adult plants, stomata closure and carbon gain from photosynthesis ceases when water content drops below 55% relative water content (RWC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Farrant, J. M. et al. A molecular physiological review of vegetative desiccation tolerance in the resurrection plant Xerophyta viscosa (Baker). Planta 242, 407–426 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2057">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Mundree, S. G. &amp; Farrant, J. M. in Plant Tolerance to Abiotic Stress in Agriculture: Role of Genetic Engineering (eds Cherry, J. H., Locy, R. D. &amp; Rychter, A. ) 201–222 (Springer, 2000)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2060">28</a></sup>. In desiccation-sensitive species, cessation of carbon gain coupled with continued water loss results in metabolic stress, whereas resurrection plants redirect their metabolism towards subcellular protection and ultimate quiescence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Gaff, D. F. &amp; Loveys, B. Abscisic acid levels in drying plants of a resurrection grass. Trans. Malaysian Soc. Plant Physiol. 3, 286–287 (1993)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2064">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Farrant, J. M., Cooper, K., Dace, H. J. W., Bentely, J. &amp; Hilgart, A. in Plant Stress Physiology (ed Shabala, S. ) 217–252 (CAB International, 2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2067">30</a></sup>. In <i>X. viscosa</i>, the molecular signature of this metabolic redirection is reflected in the higher number of genes that exhibit differential expression at 40% RWC (1.0 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt) compared to other hydrated states (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). At 40% RWC, energy metabolism is strongly repressed and transcripts of genes related to chlorophyll degradation significantly accumulated. Significant accumulation of transcripts related to chlorophyll synthesis and chloroplast and thylakoid formation were also observed during dehydration at 40% RWC (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 3</a>), reflecting the strategy of poikilochlorophylly adopted by <i>X. viscosa</i>. Poikilochlorophyllous resurrection plants dismantle their photosynthetic apparatus (thylakoids and chlorophyll) in a controlled manner during dehydration, and reconstitute this upon rehydration utilizing transcripts stably stored in desiccated tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Farrant, J. M. et al. A molecular physiological review of vegetative desiccation tolerance in the resurrection plant Xerophyta viscosa (Baker). Planta 242, 407–426 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2088">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bewley, J. D. Physiological aspects of desiccation tolerance. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. 30, 195–238 (1979)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2091">31</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Tuba, Z., Protor, M. C. F. &amp; Csintalan, Z. Ecophysiological responses of homoiochlorophyllous and poikilochlorophyllous desiccation tolerant plants: a comparison and an ecological perspective. Plant Growth Regul. 24, 211–217 (1998)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2094">33</a></sup>.</p><p>This metabolic redirection is also evident from the grouping of the differentially expressed genes in four distinct self-organizing maps (SOMs, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). From these, it is evident that two major changes in gene expression occur: between 60% RWC (1.5 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt) and 40% RWC (1.0 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt, SOMs 1 and 3) and between 40% RWC and 20% RWC (0.5 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt, SOMs 2 and 4). Genes related to protein folding, protection and translation control are enriched exclusively in SOM 1, whereas genes related to nuclear import and control of gene expression are enriched exclusively in SOM 2 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Likewise, a number of transcription factor-encoding genes and co-regulators were highly induced in desiccated leaves of the resurrection species <i>Haberlea rhodopensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gechev, T. S. et al. Molecular mechanisms of desiccation tolerance in the resurrection glacial relic Haberlea rhodopensis. Cell. Mol. Life Sci. 70, 689–709 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2122">34</a></sup>, reflecting the massive transcriptional reprogramming behind the metabolic redirection. Genes related to lipid metabolism, nucleotide metabolism and protection against oxidative stress are enriched exclusively in SOM 3, whereas genes related to energy metabolism, photosynthesis, water transport and genetic information processing are enriched exclusively in SOM 4. Decline of transcripts related to energy metabolism and photosynthesis was also observed in the resurrection species <i>H. rhodopensis</i> and <i>C. plantagineum</i> as decreasing metabolic activity is a general primary target during dehydration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gechev, T. S. et al. Molecular mechanisms of desiccation tolerance in the resurrection glacial relic Haberlea rhodopensis. Cell. Mol. Life Sci. 70, 689–709 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2133">34</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Rodriguez, M. C. S. et al. Transcriptomes of the desiccation-tolerant resurrection plant Craterostigma plantagineum. Plant J. 63, 212–228 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2136">36</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genomic organization of X. viscosa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Genomic organization of <i>X. viscosa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201738/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2017.38/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants201738_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2017.38/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants201738_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="750"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, SOMs of gene expression displaying a log<sub>2</sub> fold change of transcripts relative to full hydration. <b>b</b>, The gene co-expression network with nodes coloured according to SOMs. <b>c</b>, The co-expression network of differentially expressed genes after ABA application to the shoots (S) and roots (R) of the seedlings. <b>d</b>, A circos map with features of the five longest assembled scaffolds of the <i>X. viscosa</i> genome. The tracks from outside to inside display the gene density, the TE density, the domain density, the fold change of gene expression to the hydrated state (colour codes as in the SOMs) and the presence of putative CoDAGs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201738/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A gene co-expression network approach revealed a large overlap in the patterns of gene activation and repression in ABA-treated seedlings and drying mature leaves (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Young seedlings of <i>X. viscosa</i> are desiccation sensitive and obtain DT only later in development, but can be made tolerant by application of ABA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Thus, this overlap points to similarities between seed- and vegetative-DT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Costa, M.-C. D. et al. Key genes involved in desiccation tolerance and dormancy across life forms. Plant Sci. 251, 162–168 (2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2192">37</a></sup>. In both systems, metabolism is shut down in an orderly manner and protection mechanisms activated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Farrant, J. M., Cooper, K., Dace, H. J. W., Bentely, J. &amp; Hilgart, A. in Plant Stress Physiology (ed Shabala, S. ) 217–252 (CAB International, 2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2196">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Williams, B. et al. Trehalose accumulation triggers autophagy during plant desiccation. PLoS Genet. 11, 1–17 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2199">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Challabathula, D., Puthur, J. T. &amp; Bartels, D. Surviving metabolic arrest: photosynthesis during desiccation and rehydration in resurrection plants. Ann. NY Acad. Sci. 1365, 89–99 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2202">39</a></sup>. Moreover, upon dehydration, both in seeds and in poikilochlorophyllous resurrection species, chloroplasts are disassembled and chlorophyll degraded. All these (ABA-controlled) events have so far been considered seed-specific but here we show that vegetative-DT in Angiosperms is also regulated by ABA, employing the same genes as in seeds. This supports a hypothesis that DT was reactivated in vegetative tissues, presumably utilizing genes associated with DT in the seeds of the species or family as a ‘blueprint’, and then later modified to result in species- or family-specific DT mechanisms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gaff, D. F. &amp; Oliver, M. J. The evolution of desiccation tolerance in angiosperm plants: a rare yet common phenomenon. Funct. Plant Biol. 40, 315–328 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2207">27</a></sup>.</p><p>In the context of the gene co-expression network, the genes in SOMs 3 and 4 are more tightly co-regulated than the genes in SOMs 1 and 2, highlighting the importance of a coordinated shutdown of metabolic and cellular processes during dehydration. Accordingly, most of the network's hubs (nodes with high number of connections to other nodes, or high degree) represent genes related to energy metabolism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM153">Supplementary Data Table</a>).</p><p>The network aided the identification of ABA-independent gene expression (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Although ABA-independent signalling pathways mediated by DREB2 and DREB1/CBF have been described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Todaka, D., Shinozaki, K. &amp; Yamaguchi-Shinozaki, K. Recent advances in the dissection of drought-stress regulatory networks and strategies for development of drought-tolerant transgenic rice plants. Front. Plant Sci. 6, 84 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2227">40</a></sup>, other regulators not described yet may also be acting. Around 20% of the nodes in the network represent genes differentially expressed in response to desiccation but not in response to exogenous ABA. The gene ontology (GO) categories enriched in this group of genes are related to signalling (‘consequence of signal transduction’ and ‘generation of a signal involved in cell-cell signalling’) and transcriptional regulation (‘positive regulation of transcription regulator activity’, ‘regulation of sequence-specific DNA binding transcription factor activity’ and ‘regulation of transcription regulator activity’). These categories suggest the presence of ABA-independent regulators not described yet.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Clusters of desiccation-associated genes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Clusters of desiccation-associated genes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>Anhydrobiosis-related gene islands (ARIds) are defined as genomic regions hosting compact clusters of genes which are anhydrobiosis-related and accumulate transcripts upon desiccation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Challabathula, D., Puthur, J. T. &amp; Bartels, D. Surviving metabolic arrest: photosynthesis during desiccation and rehydration in resurrection plants. Ann. NY Acad. Sci. 1365, 89–99 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2240">39</a></sup>. In analogy with ARIds, we define clusters of desiccation-associated genes (CoDAGs) as genomic regions hosting compact clusters of genes associated with desiccation and differentially expressed upon desiccation. These regions are of interest owing to their potential role in the evolution of DT. We performed a genome-wide search for CoDAGs in <i>X. viscosa</i> considering the same criteria as for ARIds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Challabathula, D., Puthur, J. T. &amp; Bartels, D. Surviving metabolic arrest: photosynthesis during desiccation and rehydration in resurrection plants. Ann. NY Acad. Sci. 1365, 89–99 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2247">39</a></sup>: (1) they host a paralogous set of genes (containing at least a pair of genes); (2) their localization in the genome is not necessarily related to that of the potential ancestor of the expanded set of genes; and (3) all genes located within CoDAGs are differentially expressed in response to desiccation. CoDAGs are abundant in the genome of <i>X. viscosa</i> (277 CoDAGs and 600 genes involved) and are mainly composed of genes with declining transcript abundance upon dehydration (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 3</a>). The expression pattern of the genes in the same CoDAG is not necessarily the same. Genes involved in various environmental interactions form CoDAGs, such as the ABA receptor PYL (Pyrabacin Resistance-Like), ABC transporters, ethylene-responsive transcription factors, intracellular ribonucleases, LEA proteins, pathogenesis-related proteins, polyphenol oxidases and uncharacterized proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 4</a>). That the majority of CoDAGs consist of transcripts that decline upon dehydration appears contradictory, but may be related to mechanisms that suppress growth and development or energy metabolism in vegetative tissues during dehydration, and may trigger or inhibit the induction of the mechanisms that lead to vegetative DT. A less parsimonious possibility is that the genes in these CoDAGs may supress the expression of DT in vegetative tissues, allowing the induction of DT only at critical levels of water loss, and that these genes have to be suppressed in order to acquire DT.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="LEA proteins"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">LEA proteins</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>LEA proteins have been implicated in plant embryo development and maturation, response to high salinity, freezing and DT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Alpert, P. Constraints of tolerance: why are desiccation-tolerant organisms so small or rare? J. Exp. Biol. 209, 1575–1584 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2272">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Tunnacliffe, A. &amp; Wise, M. J. The continuing conundrum of the LEA proteins. Naturwissenschaften 94, 791–812 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2275">41</a></sup>. They accumulate in seeds, pollen, fungal spores, yeast cells, nematodes, rotifers and embryos of some crustaceans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Alpert, P. Constraints of tolerance: why are desiccation-tolerant organisms so small or rare? J. Exp. Biol. 209, 1575–1584 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2279">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Tunnacliffe, A. &amp; Wise, M. J. The continuing conundrum of the LEA proteins. Naturwissenschaften 94, 791–812 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2282">41</a></sup>. In resurrection plants, desiccation-induced expression of LEA transcripts is a common response as shown in <i>H. rhodopensis</i> and <i>C. plantagineum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gechev, T. S. et al. Molecular mechanisms of desiccation tolerance in the resurrection glacial relic Haberlea rhodopensis. Cell. Mol. Life Sci. 70, 689–709 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2291">34</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Rodriguez, M. C. S. et al. Transcriptomes of the desiccation-tolerant resurrection plant Craterostigma plantagineum. Plant J. 63, 212–228 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2294">36</a></sup>.</p><p>HMM (Hidden Markov Model) profiles provided by PFAM were used to search for LEAs in the genome of <i>X. viscosa</i>. We found 126 putative LEA motif-containing proteins divided into eight families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>), of which 90 were differentially expressed during drying and rehydration of adult plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). Most of the duplicated gene pairs have dispersed origins, which are paralogs that do not show conserved synteny or are not near each other on chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanx: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2310">42</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 5</a>). By performing a BLASTP search to assess the similarities between each of the LEA proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 5</a>), we found that, at the protein level, there are no repeated sequences in the genome. Thus, each LEA gene that we identified is unique, and the high number of LEAs found is due to small (punctual) duplications, and not due to the octoploid nature of the genome. The number of LEAs is significantly higher than identified in the genomes of <i>B. hygrometrica</i> (67 LEA genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xiao, L. et al. The resurrection genome of Boea hygrometrica: a blueprint for survival of dehydration. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 5833–5837 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2324">19</a></sup> and <i>O. thomaeum</i> (94 LEA genes; <a href="http://resources.oropetium.org/Oropetium_v01/">http://resources.oropetium.org/Oropetium_v01/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508–511 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2338">18</a></sup>, as well as in a phylogenetic cross-section of 25 plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Whole-genome duplications played critical roles in the expansion of LEA families in <i>X. viscosa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). In contrast, in <i>O. thomaeum</i> and <i>B. hygrometrica</i> dispersed duplications were more evident (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). A comparative genomic analysis with 25 plant species spanning the land plant phylogeny indicated that two of the eight LEA families, namely LEA_4 and LEA_6, are significantly expanded in the genome of <i>X. viscosa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 6 and Supplementary Table 5</a>). These differences might relate to the poikilochlorophyllous nature of <i>X. viscosa,</i> in contrast to the homoiochlorophyllous species <i>B. hygrometrica</i> and <i>O. thomaeum</i>. This, in turn, might relate to longevity in the dry state. Although longevity of <i>X. viscosa</i> has not been systematically assessed, two other poikilochlorophyllous species of <i>Xerophyta</i> have demonstrated longevity. Dried excised leaves of <i>Xerophyta scabrida</i> can survive up to 2 years under laboratory conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tuba, Z., Lichtenthaler, H. K., Maroti, I. &amp; Csintalan, Z. Resynthesis of thylakoids and functional chloroplasts in the desiccated leaves of the poikilochlorophyllous plant Xerophyta scabrida upon rehydration. J. Plant Physiol. 142, 742–748 (1993)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2387">43</a></sup>. In the only study reporting on longevity of whole plants in the dry state, it was demonstrated that <i>Xerophyta humilis</i> retains viability for more than 2 years, whereas the homoiochlorophyllous <i>Craterostigma wilmsii</i> lost viability within 3 months<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Bajic, J. Exploring the longevity of dry Craterostigma wilmsii (homoiochlorophyllous) and Xerophyta humilis (poikolichlorophyllous) under simulated field conditions. PhD thesis, Univ. Cape Town (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2397">44</a></sup>. Furthermore, <i>X. viscosa</i> (unlike <i>X. humilis</i>) is a chasmophyte (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>) and thus tolerates not only frequent periods of desiccation, but also extremes of temperature ranging from sub-zero to above +50 °C (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Farrant, J. M. et al. A molecular physiological review of vegetative desiccation tolerance in the resurrection plant Xerophyta viscosa (Baker). Planta 242, 407–426 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2410">9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="LEAs transcript expression and accumulation patterns during dehydration and rehydration."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: LEAs transcript expression and accumulation patterns during dehydration and rehydration.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201738/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2017.38/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants201738_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2017.38/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants201738_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="332"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The maximum likelihood tree showing the phylogenetic relationship between the 126 LEA proteins along with the gene duplication mode, and a heat map showing the log<sub>2</sub> fold change of the transcripts in each point relative to the hydrated state (2.5 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt). The dots on the branches represent bootstrap support values. <b>b</b>, The number of LEA transcripts that accumulate and decline relative to the hydrated state. The colours on the chart represent each LEA family shown in the legend. WGD, whole-genome duplication.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201738/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The response to desiccation appears LEA-family specific. Members of dehydrin (DHN), LEA_1 and LEA_4 families accumulate transcripts during drying and rehydration. It is likely that members of these LEA families are involved in a rapid primary response to dehydration and as transcripts continue to accumulate these LEA families may guarantee homeostasis in the desiccating cells. Moreover, as LEA proteins decline shortly after full rehydration, they might serve as a readily available nitrogen source to support resumption of photosynthesis and metabolism upon rehydration. Most members of LEA_2 family and a few members of DHN and LEA_3 families significantly decrease transcript abundance during drying and rehydration, suggesting that these LEAs are not involved in the protection mechanism activated by desiccation.</p><p>The LEA_2 family is the most diverse in the genome of <i>X. viscosa</i>, with 57 members. A promoter enrichment analysis showed that the LEA_2 family is activated by a more diverse range of transcription factors than other LEA families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). The ABI5 motif is prominent in the promoters of the LEA_4 family-members. The ABI5 transcription factor is mostly commonly associated with the regulation of seed maturation, and regulates transcription of LEA genes and degradation of chlorophyll essential to the establishment of seed longevity (measured by a moderate artificial aging method) in <i>Medicago truncatula</i> and <i>Pisum sativum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Verdier, J. et al. A regulatory network-based approach dissects late maturation processes related to the acquisition of desiccation tolerance and longevity of Medicago truncatula seeds. Plant Physiol. 163, 757–774 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2462">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Zinsmeister, J. et al. ABI5 is a regulator of seed maturation and longevity in legumes. Plant Cell 28, 2735–2754 (2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2465">46</a></sup>.</p><p>Predicted subcellular localization shows that LEAs can be found in seven subcellular compartments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>), supporting the diversity of LEA expression in adult leaves. Most LEA families are overrepresented in the nucleus. Seed maturation protein (SMP) family-members are overrepresented in the cytosol. LEA_2 and LEA_3 families are overrepresented in the plastids and decrease transcript abundance during drying, supporting their poikilochlorophyllous origin.</p><p>During desiccation, the most profound change in LEA expression occurs between 1.5 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt and 1.0 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt, with a 43% increase in LEA expression which was maintained during further desiccation and rehydration (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). Thus, these LEAs are largely represented by the early responses of SOMs 1 and 3 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). These observations indicate an activation of regulatory pathways essential for the desiccation response around 1.0 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt (40% RWC). This activation is likely caused by a regulatory ‘switch’ between the dehydration response (to initial water loss) and cellular preparation response (protection) for the desiccated state.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="The ABI3 regulon"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">The ABI3 regulon</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>ABI3 is a transcription factor that mediates DT in seeds through a highly conserved gene regulatory network<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Costa, M.-C. D. et al. Key genes involved in desiccation tolerance and dormancy across life forms. Plant Sci. 251, 162–168 (2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2506">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Mönke, G. et al. Toward the identification and regulation of the Arabidopsis thaliana ABI3 regulon. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, 8240–8254 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2509">47</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Khandelwal, A. et al. Role of ABA and ABI3 in desiccation tolerance. Science 327, 546 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2512">49</a></sup>. <i>X. viscosa</i> has two structural orthologues of ABI3. Due to different gene contexts (different neighbouring genes), these paralogs are not considered alleles of the same gene. They are expressed, but their expression did not change significantly in response to exogenous ABA or drying and rehydration of adult plants. We identified in <i>X. viscosa</i> 139 structural orthologues of the 98 target genes of the ABI3 regulon from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Mönke, G. et al. Toward the identification and regulation of the Arabidopsis thaliana ABI3 regulon. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, 8240–8254 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2524">47</a></sup>. The majority displayed an increase in transcript abundance in response to exogenous ABA (62% in shoots of seedlings) and to drying (45% in SOM 1). Fifty of these structural orthologues were located in our co-expression network. Although the nodes representing these genes are not directly connected, they share first neighbours, forming tightly connected sub-networks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). The GO categories enriched in these genes are primarily related to energy metabolism (‘cellular carbohydrate metabolic process’, ‘generation of precursor metabolites and energy’) and photosynthesis (‘plastid organization’, ‘regulation of photosynthesis’, ‘stomatal complex morphogenesis’ and ‘cofactor metabolic process’). Structural orthologues of seed-specific members of the ABI3 regulon were also found in our analysis, such as embryonic proteins DC-8, oleosins, seed maturation proteins and 1-Cys peroxiredoxin. Overall, these analyses shed light on the conserved mechanisms of the gene regulatory network orchestrated by ABI3 in seeds and vegetative tissues of resurrection plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Autophagy and senescence"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Autophagy and senescence</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>Autophagy is a catabolic cellular process that attempts to restore homeostasis during severe stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Williams, B. et al. Trehalose accumulation triggers autophagy during plant desiccation. PLoS Genet. 11, 1–17 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2539">38</a></sup>. It involves the recycling of nutrients and removal of damaged and potentially harmful cellular material<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Williams, B. et al. Trehalose accumulation triggers autophagy during plant desiccation. PLoS Genet. 11, 1–17 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2543">38</a></sup>. In resurrection plants, effective regulation of autophagy aids survival under extreme stress conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Williams, B. et al. Trehalose accumulation triggers autophagy during plant desiccation. PLoS Genet. 11, 1–17 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2547">38</a></sup>. Although drought-induced leaf senescence is thought to be an efficient strategy for reducing transpiration and allowing remobilization of water and nutrients, it does not occur in resurrection plants in the tissues undergoing induction of DT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Williams, B. et al. Trehalose accumulation triggers autophagy during plant desiccation. PLoS Genet. 11, 1–17 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2551">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Griffiths, C. A. et al. Drying without senescence in resurrection plants. Front. Plant Sci. 5 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2554">50</a></sup>. It is intriguing to investigate how resurrection plants regulate autophagy and avoid senescence during dehydration. On the basis of sequence similarity to proteins predicted to be involved in autophagy and senescence in the resurrection grass <i>Tripogon loliiformis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Williams, B. et al. Trehalose accumulation triggers autophagy during plant desiccation. PLoS Genet. 11, 1–17 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2560">38</a></sup>, we found a number of similar proteins in <i>X. viscosa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Table 6</a>). <i>X. viscosa</i> homologues of <i>A. thaliana AUTOPHAGY</i> (e.g. <i>ATG2, ATG8, ATG9, ATG12</i> and <i>ATG18</i>), known to delay senescence in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Li, Z., Peng, J., Wen, X. &amp; Guo, H. Gene network analysis and functional studies of senescence-associated genes reveal novel regulators of Arabidopsis leaf senescence. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 54, 526–539 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2586">51</a></sup>, cluster in SOMs 1 and 2, whereas <i>X. viscosa</i> homologues of <i>A. thaliana</i> SENESCENCE-ASSOCIATED GENE and SENESCENCE-ASSOCIATED RECEPTOR-LIKE KINASE, known to promote senescence in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Li, Z., Peng, J., Wen, X. &amp; Guo, H. Gene network analysis and functional studies of senescence-associated genes reveal novel regulators of Arabidopsis leaf senescence. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 54, 526–539 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2598">51</a></sup>, cluster in SOM4. Overall, both in <i>X. viscosa</i> and in <i>T. lolliformis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Williams, B. et al. Trehalose accumulation triggers autophagy during plant desiccation. PLoS Genet. 11, 1–17 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2608">38</a></sup> transcripts associated with senescence and pro-apoptosis exhibit a reduction in accumulation, whereas transcripts associated with the delay of senescence and anti-apoptosis accumulated during drying. Despite different strategies concerning chlorophyll retention during drying (<i>X. viscosa</i> is poikilochlorophyllous and <i>T. lolliiformis</i> is homoiochlorophyllous), processes related to autophagy and senescence appear to operate in similar ways in <i>X. viscosa</i> and <i>T. lolliiformis</i>.</p><p>Stress signalling in the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) is transduced through the unfolded protein response (UPR) pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Reis, P. A. A. et al. The binding protein BiP attenuates stress-induced cell death in soybean via modulation of the N-rich protein-mediated signaling pathway. Plant Physiol. 157, 1853–1865 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2627">52</a></sup>.
