The UPR activates the ER-located molecular chaperone binding protein BiP, that assists in folding newly synthesized proteins, acts in ER stress signalling and prevents senescence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Griffiths, C. A. et al. Drying without senescence in resurrection plants. Front. Plant Sci. 5 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2631">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Reis, P. A. A. et al. The binding protein BiP attenuates stress-induced cell death in soybean via modulation of the N-rich protein-mediated signaling pathway. Plant Physiol. 157, 1853–1865 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2634">52</a></sup>. We identified ten putative BiP genes in <i>X. viscosa</i>. Most of them clustered in SOM 2, indicating activation when the stress becomes more severe. Thus, apparently, <i>X. viscosa</i> engages a mechanism to prevent activation of the UPR cell death response during severe dehydration.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Combining genome sequence information with genome-wide gene expression data is a powerful approach to gain insight into the ‘footprint’ of DT in a resurrection species. Among the currently known resurrection plant species, <i>X. viscosa</i> is probably the most resilient<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Farrant, J. M. et al. A molecular physiological review of vegetative desiccation tolerance in the resurrection plant Xerophyta viscosa (Baker). Planta 242, 407–426 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2655">9</a></sup>. Vegetative DT was reactivated in at least 13 families of Angiosperm plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gaff, D. F. &amp; Oliver, M. J. The evolution of desiccation tolerance in angiosperm plants: a rare yet common phenomenon. Funct. Plant Biol. 40, 315–328 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2659">27</a></sup> as adaptation to environmental demands, and we propose that the basal DT mechanism was further modified according to specific environmental and habitat (niche) requirements. Reactivation of vegetative DT must have been based on the presence of genes associated with DT in reproductive structures, such as seeds, and hence the genomic information for DT was redirected towards vegetative tissues. Further genome modifications to deliver DT may have included polyploidization, as in the African Velloziaceae, during the establishment of this family on the African continent. Poikilochlorophyllous resurrection species have a clear seed imprint in their phenotype. For example, the degradation of chlorophyll during the maturation stage is common among orthodox seeds, presumably to avoid the generation of reactive oxygen species during subsequent long periods of storage in the dry state. During dismantling of photosynthetic machinery, <i>X. viscosa</i> engages anti-senescence mechanisms, giving evidence that in this species vegetative DT is uncoupled from common drought-induced senescence, again similar to seeds. In conjunction, the bZIP transcription factor ABI5 is strongly associated with the acquisition of longevity in orthodox seeds, assessed by artificial aging, as well as expression of several LEAs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Verdier, J. et al. A regulatory network-based approach dissects late maturation processes related to the acquisition of desiccation tolerance and longevity of Medicago truncatula seeds. Plant Physiol. 163, 757–774 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2666">45</a></sup>. Here we show that ABI5 may be a regulator of expression of the LEA_4 family which, consequently, may be an important factor in the longevity of <i>X. viscosa</i> in the dry state. Furthermore, we identified two structural orthologues of ABI3, a major regulator of seed maturation and DT along with the majority of the ABI3 regulon expressed in leaves. Again, this exemplifies the strong ‘seed character’ of vegetative DT in this species.</p><p>The CoDAGs that are downregulated upon dehydration may represent genes which are related to mechanisms that suppress growth and development or energy metabolism in vegetative tissues and may trigger or inhibit the induction of the mechanisms that lead to vegetative DT. The genes in these CoDAGs may also supress the expression of DT in vegetative tissues, allowing the induction of DT only at critical levels of water loss. Their down regulation may thus be part of the reactivated mechanism that evolved to deliver DT in vegetative tissues. Further study of possibly higher-order regulation of this set of genes may identify targets for modification of crops towards DT.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">DNA sequencing, <i>de novo</i> assembly and validation</h3><p>The genome of <i>X. viscosa</i> was sequenced using raw paired-end Illumina (<span class="stix">∼</span>77× coverage) and PacBio (<span class="stix">∼</span>58× coverage) technologies. Reads originating for contaminants (insects, bacterial, fungal and human), as well as chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes, were removed from all sequence data prior to assembly. Illumina reads were error corrected using Lighter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Song, L., Florea, L. &amp; Langmead, B. Lighter: fast and memory-efficient sequencing error correction without counting. Genome Biol. 15, 509 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2696">12</a></sup> and assembled using SparseAssembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ye, C. et al. Exploiting sparseness in de novo genome assembly. BMC Bioinformatics 13, S1 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2700">13</a></sup>. PacBio reads were assembled using the diploid aware FALCON assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2704">11</a></sup>. A hybrid assembly was produced with DBG2OLC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ye, C., Hill, C., Wu, S., Ruan, J. &amp; Ma, Z. DBG2OLC: efficient assembly of large genomes using long erroneous reads of the third generation sequencing technologies. Sci. Rep. 6, 31900 (2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2708">14</a></sup> and the contigs were reordered and connected into scaffolds using SSPACE-LongRead<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Boetzer, M. et al. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 211 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2713">15</a></sup>. The assembly was polished using Sparc<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ye, C. et al. Exploiting sparseness in de novo genome assembly. BMC Bioinformatics 13, S1 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2717">13</a></sup> and Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2721">53</a></sup>. PBJelly2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with pacific biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2725">54</a></sup> was used for gap closure and genome improvement. Alignments due to gene duplication and repeats were filtered out using the delta-filter utility of the MUMmer package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2729">55</a></sup>. The assembly was validated by mapping the available RNA and DNA libraries to the genome with Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2733">56</a></sup> and Blasr. Assembly statistics were calculated using QUAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Gurevich, A., Saveliev, V., Vyahhi, N. &amp; Tesler, G. QUAST: quality assessment tool for genome assemblies. Bioinformatics 29, 1072–1075 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2738">57</a></sup>. Gene space completeness was measured by using BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs). The genome assembly and validation pipeline is summarized in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM153">Supplementary Data Figure</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Annotation</h3><p>The <i>ab initio</i> predictors AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: A web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 465–467 (2005)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2756">58</a></sup> and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2760">59</a></sup> were trained on the transcriptome data using BRAKER1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Hoff, K. J., Lange, S., Lomsadze, A., Borodovsky, M. &amp; Stanke, M. BRAKER1: unsupervised RNA-Seq-based genome annotation with GeneMark-ET and AUGUSTUS. Bioinformatics 32, 767–769 (2016)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2764">60</a></sup>. The MAKER2 annotation pipeline was applied for gene prediction and repeat annotation. Predicted genes were functionally annotated by a consensus approach using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Mitchell, A. et al. The InterPro protein families database: the classification resource after 15 years. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D213–D221 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2768">23</a></sup>, Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Harris, M. A. et al. The Gene Ontology (GO) database and informatics resource. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D258–D2261 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2773">61</a></sup>, Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG), Swiss-Prot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2777">22</a></sup>, Translated EMBL Nucleotide Sequence Data Library (TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2781">22</a></sup>) and BLAST2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2785">62</a></sup>.</p><p>Repeatmodeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-3.0 (RepeatMasker, 2008); 
                    http://www.repeatmasker.org
                    
                  " href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2792">63</a></sup> was applied to build a <i>de novo</i> repeat library and identify ribosomal and small nuclear RNAs. Repetitive sequences in the assembly were soft masked using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-3.0 (RepeatMasker, 2008); 
                    http://www.repeatmasker.org
                    
                  " href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2799">63</a></sup>. Transfer RNAs (tRNAs) were annotated using tRNA-scan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: A program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2803">64</a></sup>. microRNAs were predicted using BLAST and INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933–2935 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2807">65</a></sup> against the RFAM database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Nawrocki, E. P. et al. Rfam 12.0: updates to the RNA families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D130–D137 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2812">66</a></sup>.</p><p>Single nucleotide polymorphisms and insertions and deletions (INDELs) were called relative to the genome using Freebayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Garrison, E. &amp; Marth, G. Haplotype-based variant detection from short-read sequencing. Preprint at 
                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1207.3907#
                    
                   (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2819">67</a></sup>. Detected polymorphisms from short-read alignments were discarded when the quality was below 20.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">OG inference</h3><p>Orthology between 15 plant species (<i>Amborella tricopoda</i>, <i>A. thaliana, Elaeis guineensis, E. tef, Hordeum vulgare, Musa acuminata, O. thomaeum, Oryza sativa, Phalaenopsis equestris, Phoenix dactylifera, Physcomitrella patens</i>, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza, X. viscosa, Zea mays</i> and <i>Zostera marina</i>) was determined with OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. Orthofinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2843">68</a></sup>. The proteomes were downloaded from Phytozome V11.0 (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html</a>).</p><p>Gene family expansions and contractions in comparison with other sequenced genomes were calculated for all OGs (excluding singletons). The z-score was calculated for each OG; those with z-score ≥2 represent significantly expanded gene families and those with z-score ≤−2 represent significantly contracted gene families. The number of genes per species for each OG was transformed into a matrix of z-scores. The z-score profile was hierarchically clustered using Pearson correlation as a distance measure. The functional annotation of each OG was predicted based on sequence similarity to entries in the Swiss-Prot database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2858">22</a></sup> and InterPro protein families database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Mitchell, A. et al. The InterPro protein families database: the classification resource after 15 years. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D213–D221 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2862">23</a></sup> where more than 50% of proteins in the family share the same protein annotation.</p><p>The ancestral gene content at key nodes in the phylogeny of the 15 plant species was reconstructed by Wagner parsimony with a 1.2 gain penalty using COUNT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Csuos, M. Count: evolutionary analysis of phylogenetic profiles with parsimony and likelihood. Bioinformatics 26, 1910–1912 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2869">69</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Cytogenetics</h3><p>Chromosome preparations were performed as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Szinay, D. et al. High-resolution chromosome mapping of BACs using multi-colour FISH and pooled-BAC FISH as a backbone for sequencing tomato chromosome 6. Plant J. 56, 627–637 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2881">70</a></sup>. The chromosome preparations were screened under a phase-contrast microscope and late-pachytene cells with little or no cytoplasm, good chromosome spreading and well-differentiated chromatin morphology were selected (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>).</p><p>The assessment of DNA content of <i>X. viscosa</i> was performed by Iribov (Enkhuizen, The Netherlands). Genome size was measured by flow cytometry on a CA-II cell analyser (Partec). Tetraploid <i>Brassica oleraceae</i> was used as a reference for which the DNA content was calculated as 2.8 pg.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">DT in <i>X. viscosa</i> seedlings</h3><p>Seeds were harvested from <i>X. viscosa</i> plants collected in the Buffelskloof Nature Reserve and cultivated under glasshouse conditions at the University of Cape Town (South Africa). Seeds were sown on two layers of blue filter paper (Blue Blotter Paper, Anchor Paper Company) and 50 ml of distilled water and incubated in germination cabinets with constant white light at 25 °C for <span class="stix">∼</span>10 days.</p><p>To assess DT in <i>X. viscosa</i> seedlings, we used seedlings with the first leaf between 1 and 2 mm length. Three replicates of 20 seedlings were incubated for 3 days in 6-cm Petri dishes containing 1.3 ml of solutions of distilled water (control) or 50 µM ABA on two sheets of white filter paper (grade 3hw, Biolab Products, Sartorius Stedim Biotec) in the dark at 20 °C. After incubation, seedlings were rinsed in distilled water, transferred to new Petri dishes with one sheet of white filter paper, dried and rehydrated. Drying was achieved by drying under an atmosphere of 32% relative humidity for 3 days at 20 °C, resulting in final water contents as low as 0.126 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt. Water contents were assessed gravimetrically by determination of the fresh weight and subsequently dry weight (dwt) after 17 h at 105 °C. After dehydration, seedlings were pre-humidified in humid air (100% relative humidity) for 24 h at 22 °C and rehydrated in H<sub>2</sub>O at 22 °C on a Copenhagen Table under a 12/12 h dark/light regime. DT was quantified as percentage of seedlings that exhibited growth resumption with both green leaves and development of a root system (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201738#MOESM152">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><p>For RNA extraction, seedlings treated with ABA and non-treated were dissected in three parts: seed coat, shoots and roots. The seed coat was discarded and shoots and roots were used for RNA extraction. Total RNA was extracted following a modified hot borate protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Wan, C.-Y. &amp; Wilkins, T. A. A modified hot borate method significantly enhances the yield of high quality RNA from cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L.). Anal. Biochem. 223, 7–12 (1994)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2931">71</a></sup>.</p><p>The samples were sequenced (150 nt, single-end) with Illumina HighSeq 2,000 and 55.2 Gb were obtained after trimming (12 libraries). A <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly was constructed using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2941">72</a></sup>. The raw cDNA sequencing (RNA-seq) data have been deposited in NCBI SRA database under accession SRS1071017.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">DT in adult plants</h3><p>A population of 80 clonally propagated 5-month-old <i>X. viscosa</i> plants were grown in Premier Pro-Mix BX (Premier Tech Horticulture Pennsylvania, USA) under the following greenhouse conditions: 16-hour days at 27 °C during the day and 18 °C at night. Prior to the initiation of the drying period, well-watered plants were covered with a plastic bag overnight to maximize the hydrated water contents prior to sampling. Each of the 80 plants were treated as individual biological reps for sampling. Dehydration was achieved by withholding water from the pots. Young green leaf tissue was harvested from 16 individual plants which were sampled daily to monitor their total water content (TWC). A portion of the tissue from each of the 16 plants was flash-frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at −80 °C. Turgid total water fraction (TWC), expressed as grams of water per gram of DWT, was determined gravimetrically using the remainder of the sample and calculated as TWC = (FWT – DWT)/DWT. DWT of each sample was determined gravimetrically after exposure to 70 °C in a convection oven until constant weight, which took 4 h<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Oliver, M. J. et al. A sister group contrast using untargeted global metabolomic analysis delineates the biochemical regulation underlying desiccation tolerance in Sporobolus stapfianus. Plant Cell 23, 1231–1248 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2956">73</a></sup>. Plants were rehydrated by fully saturating the soil and aerially by misting with water.</p><p>Triplicate samples were selected with TWCs of 2.5 (fully hydrated TWC), 2, 1.5, 1, 0.5 and 0.1 gH<sub>2</sub>O g<sup>−1</sup> dwt and rehydrated tissues at 12 and 24 h post-re-watering were selected for RNA extraction. RNA was extracted using the RNeasy (Qiagen, Hilden, Germany) kit with the RLC buffer following the manufacturer's recommended protocol. The RNA isolates were treated with DNase1 and cleaned using the DNA-free RNA Kit (Zymo Technologies, Irvine, CA). RNA quality was assessed by use of a fragment analyser (Advanced Analytical Technologies, Ankeny, IA) and concentration determined by use of a Nanodrop Spectrophotometer (ThermoFisher, Waltham, MA).</p><p>RNA libraries were created and individually bar-coded from 2.7 µg of template total RNA utilizing the TruSeq RNA Sample Prep Kit (Illumina, San Diego, CA) as described in the manufacturer's recommended protocol. Libraries were pooled in groups of six and sequenced (six samples per lane) on an Illumina HiSeq 2,500 ultra-high-throughput DNA sequencing platform (Illumina, San Diego, CA) at the DNA Core facility at the University of Missouri, Columbia, MI, USA (<a href="http://dnacore.missouri.edu//HiSeq.html">http://dnacore.missouri.edu//HiSeq.html</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene expression analyses</h3><p>Gene expression was calculated using TopHat and Cufflinks from the Tuxedo suite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2985">74</a></sup>. Differential expression was then computed using Cuffdiff, also from the Tuxedo suite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d41924e2989">74</a></sup>. The expression of 12 genes was analysed by quantitative PCR (qPCR) in shoots in order to verify the accuracy of the data. Candidate reference genes were chosen based on the RNA-Seq data applying a cut-off of ≥0.9 for <i>q</i>-value. These genes were further checked using qBase+ (Biogazelle) applying a cut-off of ≤0.5 for M-value (gene stability value) and ≤0.15 for CV-value (coefficient of variation), generating a shortlist of seven reference genes. The three reference genes with most stable expression in the qPCR were used for expression normalization of the target genes. Both RNA-Seq and qPCR showed comparable trends.</p><p>The annotated genome was used for an over-representation analysis to recover over-represented biological processes (using Benjamini &amp; Hochberg False Discovery Rate correction, <i>P</i> value ≤0.05) based on gene ontologies using the plugin BiNGO for Cytoscape. InterProScan and protein BLAST (using <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>Z. mays</i> as a reference) were used to assign GO-terms to the total set of annotated genes. The whole annotation was used as reference set. The term's semantic distance with respect to other semantically close terms (‘Dispensability’) was calculated using the online tool ReviGO (<a href="http://revigo.irb.hr/">http://revigo.irb.hr/</a>) and used to remove redundant terms applying a cut-off of ≤0.05.</p><p>SOMs were calculated using GeneMaths software (version 2.1, Applied Maths BVBA, Sint-Martens-Latem, Belgium) by importing gene expression data and mapping them into four groups (2 × 2 node format) that provided optimal representation of gene expression patterns in a small number of independent bins.</p><p>Orthologues were defined as hits with lowest Expect value (E-value), with a threshold of ≤10<sup>−10</sup>. Multiple hits were considered orthologues when the difference between their E-values and the lowest hit's E-value was smaller than 10<sup>−10</sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Identification of LEA proteins</h3><p>To identify members of LEA protein families, all HMMs from the PFAM database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Finn, R. D. et al. The Pfam protein families database: towards a more sustainable future. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D279–D285 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d41924e3036">75</a></sup> (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org">http://pfam.xfam.org</a>) were uploaded and used to generate HMM profile matrix using the program hmmbuild of the HMMER3.0 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Finn, R. D., Clements, J. &amp; Eddy, S. R. HMMER web server: interactive sequence similarity searching. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, W29–W37 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d41924e3047">76</a></sup>. The HMM profiles were used to identify members of the eight LEA families (DHN - <a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/family/PF00257">PF00257</a>, LEA_1 - <a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/family/PF03760">PF03760</a>, LEA_2 - <a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/family/PF03168">PF03168</a>, LEA_3 - <a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/family/PF03242">PF03242</a>, LEA_4 - <a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/family/PF02987">PF02987</a>, LEA_5 - <a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/family/PF00477">PF00477</a>, LEA_6 - <a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/family/PF10714">PF10714</a> and SMP - <a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/family/PF04927">PF04927</a>) in the genome of <i>X. viscosa</i> using the program hmmscan. All proteins with significant hits (E-value &lt;0.01) were collected.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Expansion of <i>X. viscosa</i> LEA families</h3><p>We collected protein sequences from 24 species (<i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>B. hygrometrica, Brachypodium distachyon</i>, <i>Brachypodium stacei</i>, <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i>, <i>E. guineensis, E. tef</i>, <i>H. vulgare, M. acuminata</i>, <i>O. thomaeum, O. sativa</i>, <i>Panicum hallii</i>, <i>P. equestris, P. dactylifera, P. patens</i>, <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i>, <i>Setaria italica</i>, <i>Setaria viridis</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor, Sphagnum fallax</i>, <i>S. polyrhiza</i>, <i>Z. mays</i> and <i>Z. marina</i>). Protein sequences were retrieved in the same way as described above for <i>X. viscosa</i>, using HMM from PFAM and searching with HMMER3.0 program.</p><p>LEA family expansions were calculated as described above. The families that presented z-score ≥2 were considered significantly expanded.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Phylogenetic and gene duplication analysis</h3><p>Multiple sequence alignment of the 126 putative LEAs was generated using MAFFT version 7 (<a href="http://mafft.cbrc.jp/alignment/server/">http://mafft.cbrc.jp/alignment/server/</a>) with default parameters. The test for the best amino acid substitution model and maximum likelihood tree were performed by IQ-Tree with default parameters, and the model WAG+I+G4 was selected according to Bayesian information criterion and 1,000 ultra-rapid bootstraps. The phylogenetic tree was edited and displayed using the online application iTOL v3.0 (Interactive Tree of Life, <a href="http://itol.embl.de/">http://itol.embl.de/</a>).</p><p>For the comparative analysis with 24 species, multiple sequence alignments of the significantly expanded LEA_4 and LEA_6 families were performed using MAFFT with default parameters and trimmed with the online tool TrimAl v1.3 (<a href="http://trimal.cgenomics.org/">http://trimal.cgenomics.org/</a>) implemented in Phylemon v2.0 with settings: conserving at least 50% of the positions and gap threshold 0.7. Maximum likelihood analysis and amino acid substitution model tests were performed with IQ-Tree with default parameters and 1,000 ultra-rapid bootstraps. The model JTT+F+G4 was selected for LEA_4 family and JTTDCMut+G4 for LEA_6 according to Bayesian information criterion. iTOL v3.0 was used for editing and displaying the phylogenetic trees. We used the ‘duplicate_gene_classifier’ command in MCScanX package to determine the origins of duplicate genes for the LEA families in <i>X. viscosa</i> genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Promoter motif analysis</h3><p>The MEME program available within the web tool MEME-Lab (<a href="http://meme-suite.org/tools/meme">http://meme-suite.org/tools/meme</a>) was used for discovery of motifs at the 1 kb region upstream of each LEA family. The parameters used were: occurrence per sequence 1, motif count 10, motif width between 6 wide and 15 wide (inclusive). Subsequently, the motifs found were compared with annotated motifs in the Jaspar Core 2016 Plants database by Tomtom, which is part of the MEME suite platform, with default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Protein structure and subcellular localization</h3><p>We used PSI program (Plant Subcellular-localization Integrative predictor, <a href="http://bis.zju.edu.cn/psi/">http://bis.zju.edu.cn/psi/</a>) to predict the subcellular localization of LEAs of <i>X. viscosa</i> proteins. PSI was chosen because it integrates the main predictors such as Cello, mPloc, Predotar, mitoProt, MultiLoc, TargetP, Wolf PSORT, subcellPredict, iPsort, Yloc, PTS1. The grand average of hydropathy (GRAVY) value for protein sequences calculation was performed with GRAVY calculator (<a href="http://www.gravy-calculator.de/">http://www.gravy-calculator.de/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Gene co-expression network</h3><p>Genes differentially expressed in at least one comparison between a time-point in the dehydration/rehydration curve and the hydrated state were used. Pearson correlation coefficients between all pairs of differentially expressed genes were calculated. A table with correlation coefficient values was exported to Cytoscape v.2.8.2 and correlation coefficients above a threshold of 0.96 (determined according to ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Freeman, T. C. et al. Construction, visualisation, and clustering of transcription networks from microarray expression data. PLoS Comput. Biol. 3, 2032–2042 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201738#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d41924e3267">77</a>) were used to filter the connections and determine the edges between nodes in the network. The resulting network was displayed with a yGraph Organic layout. The Cytoscape built-in app NetworkAnalyzer was used to compute network parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Data availability</h3><p>Sequence and transcriptome data are available from the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) under BioSample ID <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA291133">PRJNA291133</a> and BioProject ID <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/?term=SAMN03940242">SAMN03940242</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec23" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec23-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec23">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec23-content"><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Costa, M.-C. D. <i>et al.</i> A footprint of desiccation tolerance in the genome of <i>Xerophyta viscosa. Nat. Plants</i> <b>3</b>, 17038 (2017).</p><p><b>Publisher's note:</b> Springer Nature remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional affiliations.</p></div></div></section>

Increasing drought under global warming in observations and models. <i>Nat. Clim.                     class="c-article-further-reading__authors-list js-list-authors-3 js-etal-collapsed" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"
                    id="authors-3">
                  <li><span
                        class="js-separator"></span><span>Melvin J. Oliver</span></li><li><span

              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-3">

              </p>
            </li>
